Brewster v. Maryland Sec. Comm'r


No. 102, September Term, 1988


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


76 Md. App. 722; 548 A.2d 157; 1988 Md. App. LEXIS 197



October 6, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended October 17, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City Kathleen O. Friedman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant securities salesperson sought review of a judgment from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which affirmed appellee commissioner's suspension of her license for 30 days and imposed a fine of $ 100 for "dishonest and unethical practices." The securities salesperson claimed that the statute was unconstitutionally vague and violated her First Amendment rights.


OVERVIEW: The securities salesperson recommended a securities purchase to a client without warning him of the risks, and induced the client to inflate his net worth five times in order to obtain approval of the trade. After the agreement to purchase was made, the securities salesperson sent the client a prospectus and the client changed his mind. The commission found that the securities salesperson was guilty of "dishonest and unethical practices" in violation of Md. Code Ann. Corps. & Ass'ns §11-412(a)(7), and the trial court affirmed the finding. The securities salesperson sought review and the court affirmed the judgment. The court held that: (1) the penal statute did not violate due process as being vague and uncertain because it had to be construed together with Md. Regs. Code § 02.02.02.02B and National Assn. of Securities Dealers (NASD) R. of Fair Practice art. III, § 2, which embodied the ethical rules; and (2) commercial free speech had to concern lawful activity and could not be misleading, and could be restricted against communication that was more likely to deceive the public than to inform it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that validated the commissioner's suspension of the securities salesperson's license and fine, with costs to be paid by the securities salesperson.

 

1124 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Coley v. State


No. 113, September Term, 1988


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


76 Md. App. 731; 548 A.2d 161; 1988 Md. App. LEXIS 198



October 6, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM THE Circuit Court for Baltimore City, Robert I. H. Hammerman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: SENTENCE VACATED; CASE REMANDED TO CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY FOR RESENTENCING IN ACCORDANCE WITH THIS OPINION; MAYOR AND CITY COUNCIL OF BALTIMORE TO PAY THE COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which, on remand, sentenced him to life imprisonment for his conviction for felony murder.


OVERVIEW: After a joint trial with two co-defendants, defendant was convicted of felony murder. The trial court sentenced the co-defendants to life in prison, the maximum sentence the law allowed. The trial court, however, sentenced defendant to life in prison with 15 years of it suspended. Defendant appealed. The court affirmed defendant's conviction but vacated his sentence. The case was remanded for resentencing, at which the trial court sentenced defendant to life in prison with no time suspended. The court held that the trial court illegally increased defendant's sentence by reinstating the suspended portion of his life sentence after remand. The record of the initial sentencing hearing clearly showed that the trial court intended to sentence defendant to something less than life in prison. Applying Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 12-702(b), the resentencing was improper in that it increased defendant's sentence solely because of his partially successful appeal and not due to some identifiable conduct on the part of defendant, which was either criminal or reprehensible behavior.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the sentence and remanded the cause to the trial court for resentencing. On resentencing, the trial court was directed to take evidence and make a finding of defendant's life expectancy, to deduct 15 years from that life expectancy, and impose a life sentence with all but that net amount, life expectancy less 15 years, suspended.

 

1125 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Robinson v. Pleet


No. 1650, September Term, 1987


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


76 Md. App. 173; 544 A.2d 1; 1988 Md. App. LEXIS 147



July 14, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied October 11, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE Circuit Court for Baltimore County, Edward A. DeWaters, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED; APPELLANT TO PAY THE COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant sought review of a judgment entered by the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland), which granted appellee health care providers' motion to dismiss because the claimant did not file a certificate of qualified expert with the Health Claims Arbitration Office (HCAO) within the time period prescribed by a provision of the Health Claims Act (Act), Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 3-2A-04(b)(1) (Cum. Supp. 1987).


OVERVIEW: The claimant filed a medical malpractice claim against the health care providers with the HCAO. Her claim was dismissed by the chairperson of the arbitration panel because she failed to file a certificate of qualified expert within 90 days as was required by § 3-2A-04(b)(1). The HCAO Director entered an award in favor of the health care providers. Thereafter, the claimant commenced an action, which sought to nullify and vacate that award. The trial court dismissed her complaint. She appealed that judgment, which was affirmed. Under the Act, the submission of malpractice actions to the HCAO was a condition precedent to the institution of a court action. Moreover, compliance with that procedure prior to the commencement of a court action was akin to the need to exhaust administrative remedies prior to seeking judicial review. Moreover, the fact that the certificate required by § 3-2A-04(b)(1) was filed with the trial court did not revive a claim. Because a provision of the Act, Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 3-2A-04(b)(5) (Cum. Supp. 1987), authorized extensions, the Act did not abridge or otherwise interfere with the claimant's constitutional right to a jury trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which dismissed the claimant's malpractice action against the health care providers.

Friel v. Triangle Oil Co.


No. 1520 September Term, 1987


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


76 Md. App. 96; 543 A.2d 863; 1988 Md. App. LEXIS 155



July 11, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended August 5, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM THE Circuit Court for Queen Anne's County, Clayton C. Carter, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED; CASE REMANDED FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION; COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLEE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant cannery sought to overturn the judgment of the Circuit Court for Queen Anne's County (Maryland), which affirmed the County Board of Appeals' decision approving the issuance of a building permit to appellee oil company. The Board of Appeals had affirmed the decision of the County Planning Commission.


OVERVIEW: The county passed a nine month moratorium on zoning, so that its zoning procedures could be revised. The oil company was adjacent to the cannery, and the oil company received an exemption from the moratorium to construct an oil storage facility. In granting the exemption, neither the county planning commission nor the board of appeals considered the aesthetics of the oil storage facility. The lower court held that the moratorium was void because it unconstitutionally deprived the oil company of its property and approved the building permit. The court reversed and remanded, holding that there was no need to address the issue of whether the oil company's property had been taken by the moratorium because the oil company was not seeking compensation for a taking. The court ruled that Queen Anne's County, Md., Comprehensive Zoning Ordinance § 17.161 required the consideration of aesthetic factors in reviewing site plans, and the failure of the administrative bodies to consider aesthetics violated § 17.161 and necessitated a remand by the lower court to the planning commission for consideration of aesthetic issues.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's decision, which approved the granting of an exemption from the zoning moratorium so that the oil company could construct an oil storage facility. The court remanded the matter back to the planning commission to consider the aesthetics of the oil company's storage facility.

Argabright v. State


No. 1396, September Term, 1987


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


75 Md. App. 442; 541 A.2d 1017; 1988 Md. App. LEXIS 127



June 7, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM THE Circuit Court for Howard County, J. Thomas Nissel, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS REVERSED. CASE REMANDED TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR HOWARD COUNTY FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS TO BE PAID BY HOWARD COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant, found to have violated the terms of his probation by the Circuit Court for Howard County (Maryland), appealed that finding on the grounds that his right to counsel was violated.


OVERVIEW: Criminal defendant appealed a finding that he had violated the conditions of his probation, contending that he was improperly denied his right to the assistance of counsel at the revocation hearing. The court reversed and remanded. Defendant had been brought before the trial judge prior to trial, and expressed his desire to dismiss his public defender. Although defendant noted that he did not wish to represent himself, he stated that he was resigned to doing so dismissal of his public defender left him with no alternative. In fact, this was incorrect. The state procedural rules provided that if the dismissal was meritorious and the individual charged was indigent, an attorney unaffiliated with the public defender's office could be appointed free of charge. The case was therefore remanded so that the court could determine whether defendant's dismissal decision was meritorious or meritless, and if meritorious whether defendant was or was not indigent.


OUTCOME: The judgment below was reversed, and the case was remanded for further proceedings. The trial judge erred when he failed to determine that defendant's dismissal of his public defender warranted denying him counsel altogether, for it was unclear if that dismissal was for a proper or improper reason.

1130 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Schochet v. State


No. 864, September Term, 1987


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


75 Md. App. 314; 541 A.2d 183; 1988 Md. App. LEXIS 109



May 19, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County, Irma Raker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED; COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which convicted defendant of an unnatural and perverted sexual practice, specifically, fellatio, as prohibited by Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 554, and which sentenced defendant to a suspended term of five years incarceration and placed defendant on supervised probation for five years.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with rape, fellatio, and anal intercourse. The jury convicted defendant of an unnatural and perverted sexual practice, which was fellatio, in violation of Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 554, but it acquitted defendant of the other charges. The trial court sentenced defendant to five years imprisonment, but it suspended the sentence and placed defendant on supervised probation for five years. Defendant argued that § 554 was unconstitutional as applied to private, noncommercial sexual acts between consenting heterosexual adults. The court affirmed, finding that it could not overturn the presumptively valid legislative action on the ground that it violated an ostensible constitutional protection, because there was no affirmative evidence that such a constitutional protection existed. There was no holding by the U.S. Supreme Court that the right of privacy applied to conduct of the type prohibited by § 554. If social changes had rendered the sodomy statutes unsuitable, it was the legislative, not the judicial, branch that was to enact the change in the statute. The sentence was not improper because the maximum sentence was 10 years imprisonment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that convicted defendant of an unnatural and perverted sexual practice, specifically, fellatio, and that sentenced defendant to a term of five years incarceration, which the trial court suspended.

Maryland State Retirement & Pension Systems v. Martin


No. 1434, September Term, 1987


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


75 Md. App. 240; 540 A.2d 1188; 1988 Md. App. LEXIS 99



May 11, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM THE Circuit Court for Garrett County, Daniel W. Moylan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR GARRETT COUNTY FOR ENTRY OF ORDER AFFIRMING THE BOARD OF TRUSTEES FOR THE MARYLAND STATE RETIREMENT AND PENSION SYSTEMS. COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE APPELLEE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Board of Trustees for the Maryland state Retirement and Pension Programs (board), challenged an order from the Circuit Court for Garrett County (Maryland), which reversed the board's decision that held that appellee employee could not revoke his transfer of funds from the retirement system to the pension system and denied the employee's request for reinstatement into the retirement system.


OVERVIEW: The employee was a member of the retirement system, provided by Md. Ann. Code art. 73B, § 2 et seq. He then transferred to the pension fund, provided for by Md. Ann. Code art. 73B, § 112 et seq., and executed a waiver as required by Md. Ann. Code art. 73B, § 3(8). He received a refund of his contribution and ceased contributing a portion of his salary to the retirement account. A few months later, the board denied the employee's requested reinstatement into the retirement system on the ground that a transfer to the pension system was irrevocable. On appeal of the circuit court's reversal, the court held that the circuit court improperly raised, developed, and ruled upon issues not presented by the parties or raised before the board. The circuit court abused its discretion by engaging in independent fact-finding when it examined the pension system transfer form and held that it was invalid as a contract. The circuit court also erred by re-evaluating the credibility of testimony from the employee and a retirement counselor from the board when the board had already made its findings based on inconsistencies in the employee's representations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the circuit court's order that reversed the board's determination and required the board to reinstate the employee in the retirement system and transfer his funds back from the pension system. The court remanded the action to the circuit court for the entry of an order affirming the board's decision.

Cassidy v. State


No. 297, September Term, 1987


COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS OF MARYLAND


74 Md. App. 1; 536 A.2d 666; 1988 Md. App. LEXIS 41



February 3, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended February 11, 1988. Certiorari Denied May 31, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Prince George's County, Audrey E. Melbourne, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS REVERSED; CASE REMANDED FOR NEW TRIAL; COSTS TO BE PAID BY PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court for Prince George's County (Maryland) convicted defendant of child abuse and assault. Defendant appealed his conviction.


OVERVIEW: The two-year-old victim, when asked by a doctor three days after the incident who had caused her bruises, on each occasion said, "Daddy." Defendant claimed on appeal that the statements were inadmissible hearsay. The State contended that they were statements of bodily feeling made to a treating physician, an excited utterance, or res gestae. The court noted that affirmative evidence of trustworthiness contemplated more than the absence of evidence of untrustworthiness. The court determined that whatever could have been analyzed under one or another of the forms of res gestae could be analyzed more cleanly in a water-tight compartment of its own. The court found precedent that would have admitted into evidence a child abuse victim's identification of its abuser made to a physician totally unpersuasive. The court determined that the out-of-court assertion, "Daddy did this," was not remotely an excited utterance, that it was not a statement of bodily condition made to a treating physician, and that a priori, it was not part of some ill-defined thing once called res gestae. The court concluded that its admission into evidence was in error, and that the error was not harmless.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgments convicting defendant, and remanded the case for a new trial.

 

1133 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Treece v. State


No. 124, September Term, 1987


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


72 Md. App. 644; 532 A.2d 175; 1987 Md. App. LEXIS 399



November 5, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Prince George's County, Howard S. Chasanow, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the Circuit Court for Prince George's County (Maryland) for second degree rape and false imprisonment. Defendant was sentenced to 15 years on the second degree rape conviction, and the false imprisonment conviction was merged.


OVERVIEW: Over defendant's objection, a plea of not criminally responsible was entered by defense counsel. The court found that the record indicated that defendant's opposition to the imposition of the not criminally responsible plea was to buttress his habeas corpus motion in which he claimed that he was denied a speedy trial. The court found that the trial court properly allowed defense counsel to proceed on the theory that defendant was not criminally responsible at the time of the offense where the trial court found ample basis for the plea after hearing testimony of psychiatrists for defendant and for the State. Because defendant was a party, through his counsel, to the postponement request and because the postponement was for the purpose of having competency determined, the court held that the postponement did not violate Md. R. 4-271. Similarly, there was no violation of defendant's Constitutional right to a speedy trial. The court found that Md. Code Ann., Health-Gen. § 12-109(b), which provided that defendant had the burden to establish the defense of not criminally responsible, did not relieve the State of its burden to prove mens rea and was not, therefore, unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction.

 

1134 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


State Administrative Bd. of Election Laws v. Billhimer


No. 131, September Term, 1987


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


72 Md. App. 578; 531 A.2d 1298; 1987 Md. App. LEXIS 392



October 9, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County, James C. Cawood, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the State Administrative Board of Election Laws (SABEL), sought review of a decision of the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County (Maryland), which reversed an administrative decision that appellee employee was properly terminated as deputy administrator of SABEL as an unclassified employee.


OVERVIEW: The employee asserted that he was a classified employee and could not be terminated without cause. The lower court found that the conclusion reached by the agency was not supported by evidence demonstrating that the statutory criteria for exemption from classified service had been satisfied. The court agreed and held that the statutory language was clear and unambiguous. Under Md. Ann. Code art. 64A, § 1, all employees of the State were deemed "classified" unless they had been specifically exempted from that category. Although Md. Ann. Code art. 64A, § 3(13) authorized the creation of the deputy administrator's job as an unclassified position, four criteria had to be met. The findings on which the decision of the agency grounded its determination were insubstantial and unrelated to the statutory mandate of art. 64A, § 3. It was not clear, as the arbitrator stated, that the deputy administrator position was one requiring definite and specific expertise. Even if the employee knew that he was "coded" as an unclassified employee, the unvarying construction of a law by the agency charged with its enforcement over a long period of time could not override the plain meaning of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Cahill v. Montgomery County


No. 1680 September Term, 1986


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


72 Md. App. 274; 528 A.2d 527; 1987 Md. App. LEXIS 368



July 16, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied December 9, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County, L. Leonard Ruben, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED IN PART AND AFFIRMED IN PART; CASE REMANDED TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR MONTGOMERY COUNTY FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS NOT INCONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS TO BE PAID ONE-THIRD BY APPELLANT AND TWO-THIRDS BY APPELLEE MONTGOMERY COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant resident sought review of a decision of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which granted a summary judgment motion, in the resident's 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 action, in favor of appellees, an animal control officer, the director of animal control, and the county.


OVERVIEW: An animal control officer was told that the resident had 40 dogs on her premises. After learning that the resident had only three dogs licensed, the officer, who knew that the resident ran an animal rescue, sought an order permitting entry, ostensibly for the purpose of conducting a physical inspection to determine whether unvaccinated dogs were present. The reviewing court authorized the entry and search, which uncovered no violations of the animal control laws. The resident brought a § 1983 action alleging that her Fourth Amendment rights had been violated. The trial court granted the summary judgment motion of appellees. On appeal, the court affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded the action. The court held that: (1) the officer did not have probable cause to search the resident's home; (2) the officer and director of animal control were protected by qualified immunity and were not liable for damages; (3) the immunity defense affected only the award of monetary relief and did not affect the resident's entitlement to injunctive or declaratory relief; and (4) the cause was remanded for consideration of whether the county was liable because of its policies.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the decision of the trial court. It reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment to the officer, the director, and the county, and remanded the case for further proceedings consistent with its opinion.

 

1136 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Tibbs v. State


No. 1618, September Term, 1986


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


72 Md. App. 239; 528 A.2d 510; 1987 Md. App. LEXIS 369



July 16, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied December 9, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City, Arrie Davis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which found defendant guilty of, inter alia, murder. Defendant contended that the trial court erred by overruling his objection to a witness's testimony and denying a mistrial when evidence of a conversation with his attorney was disclosed, and that the prosecutor's repeated misconduct was so prejudicial as to deny defendant a fair trial and due process.


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to his conviction for, inter alia, murder and conspiracy to rob, defendant contended that the trial court committed reversible error, that a mistrial was warranted, and that he was denied due process and a fair trial. The court affirmed the conviction and held that: (1) while a witness's testimony regarding conversations with defendant were hearsay for which there was no exception, there was no reversible error, as the trial court offered a curative instruction, which defendant rejected, and because the error was harmless due to the overwhelming evidence against defendant; (2) evidence of a conversation between defendant and his attorney did not require a mistrial in the absence of extraordinary circumstances or manifest necessity, and the discussion inferred no clear prejudice to defendant; and (3) defendant was not denied due process and a fair trial by the prosecutor's numerous improper statements and questions, as the statements were cured by the trial court's instructions to the jury, and it did not appear that the jury was misled or influenced to defendant's prejudice because the case against defendant was strong.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant for murder, attempted robbery with a deadly weapon, conspiracy to rob, and certain handgun violations.

Sheetz v. Baltimore


No. 1443, September Term, 1986


COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS OF MARYLAND


72 Md. App. 51; 527 A.2d 787; 1987 Md. App. LEXIS 355



July 9, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Granted December 10, 1987.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED; COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant correctional officer sought review of a judgment of the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which affirmed a decision of the Baltimore City Civil Service Commission (commission) in favor of appellees, mayor and city. The commission sustained a warden's decision to terminate the correctional officer's employment.


OVERVIEW: The police arrested the correctional officer after they noticed the trafficking of people to and from his van. In a warrantless search incident to the arrest, they seized drugs and drug paraphernalia. The correctional officer was charged with possession, but the evidence seized was suppressed on the ground that the search was in violation of the correctional officer's fourth amendment rights. The warden of the city jail initiated a civil administrative proceeding. The correctional officer's employment was terminated. The commission and the circuit court affirmed the termination. On appeal, the court declined to apply the exclusionary rule and affirmed the judgment of the circuit court. The court noted that the rule had not been extended to proceedings that were not purely punitive. The court determined that the costs of applying the rule to the correctional officer's situation clearly exceeded any added deterrence that the rule's application would bring. The court found that application of the rule would adversely affect the future operation of the city jail, and concluded that the public interest in ensuring the safe and efficient operation of prisons took precedence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the circuit court, which affirmed the decision of the commission that the correctional officer's employment could be terminated by the mayor and the city.

 

1138 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Chew v. State


No. 1067, September Term, 1986


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


71 Md. App. 681; 527 A.2d 332; 1987 Md. App. LEXIS 349



July 7, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Charles County, George W. Bowling, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS VACATED AND CASE REMANDED FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS; IF COURT FINDS NO EQUAL PROTECTION VIOLATION, JUDGMENTS ARE AFFIRMED; IF COURT FINDS AN EQUAL PROTECTION VIOLATION, JUDGMENTS ARE REVERSED AND CASE TO BE RETRIED ON THE MERITS; COSTS TO BE PAID BY CHARLES COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction from the Circuit Court for Charles County (Maryland), for first-degree felony-murder, an attempt to commit rape in the first degree, and a third-degree sexual offense because the state's use of preemptory challenges in the jury selection process violated the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a black man, sexually assaulted and murdered a young woman. He was convicted of first-degree felony-murder, an attempt to commit rape in the first degree, and a third-degree sexual offense. Among other things, defendant argued on appeal that the state's use of peremptory challenges to strike every member of defendant's race from the jury panel violated his constitutional right to the equal protection of the law. The court vacated and remanded for further proceedings. The court held that defendant raised an inference of discrimination by showing that the state exercised peremptory challenges to remove three black jurors from the jury. Because the trial court failed to make a ruling on the discriminatory use of the preemptory challenges, a reversal was not mandated. If, upon remand, the trial court ruled that defendant did not establish a prima facie case of discrimination or the state provided satisfactorily neutral explanations to rebut the prima facie case, defendant's convictions were to stand.


OUTCOME: The court vacated defendant's convictions and remanded for further proceedings. The judgments were to be affirmed if there was no equal protection violation and reversed if there was an equal protection violation in the use of the preemptory challenges in the jury selection.

Carroll County v. Raymond I. Richardson Foundation, Inc.


No. 1183, September Term, 1986


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


71 Md. App. 434; 526 A.2d 81; 1987 Md. App. LEXIS 328



June 5, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Carroll County, Donald J. Gilmore, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED; COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of a judgment of the Circuit Court for Carroll County (Maryland), which reversed a decision by the Carroll County Board of Zoning Appeals holding that appellee juvenile facility was not a "school" within the meaning of a zoning ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The juvenile facility provided education, counseling, and vocational training to juveniles with troubled backgrounds. The board determined that the juvenile facility was not a "school" within the meaning of Carroll County, Md., Zoning Ordinance art. 6, § 6.2(i) (1985), which permitted "schools" within an otherwise agricultural district. The juvenile facility filed an action challenging the board's decision. The circuit court reversed the board's decision, holding that the juvenile facility was a "school" within the meaning of the ordinance. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the circuit court. The court held that non-traditional types of schools were not excluded from the district merely because they were not specifically provided for in the ordinance. The court determined that the juvenile facility was a "school" within the meaning of the ordinance because it employed teachers, provided educational instruction that was coordinated with public school systems, and was generally focused on the education of its residents.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the circuit court's judgment holding that the juvenile facility was a "school" within the meaning of the zoning ordinance.

 

1141 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Burns v. Rockville


No. 947, September Term, 1986


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


71 Md. App. 293; 525 A.2d 255; 1987 Md. App. LEXIS 317



May 11, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County, Calvin R. Sanders, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a husband and wife, filed an action seeking damages for personal injuries that the wife sustained when she fill into an orchestra pit while attending a civic ballet performance at a civic center. The Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), on sovereign immunity grounds, granted the motion for summary judgment filed by appellees, a mayor and city council. Appellants sought review.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held that when the civic ballet performed it was engaged in a governmental rather than a proprietary function. On appeal, the court affirmed the summary judgment in appellees' favor. The court held that whether the ballet made a profit was not a factual question. Even it the challenged expenses were omitted it was still obvious that the ballet operated at a loss. The court rejected appellants' argument that attempted to divide a single municipal activity into two components, one governmental and one proprietary. On the night the wife was injured, the theater was being used by a city authorized and funded ballet for the purpose of entertaining the public at large. Because the program was legislatively authorized, for the public benefit with little or no dollar profit or emolument to the city, benefited the public health, education and welfare and had no element of private interest, the trial court correctly concluded that the nature of the activity was governmental. A waiver of immunity must have been legislatively authorized, and could not have been presumed. The court refused to abolish the doctrine of immunity as applied to municipal corporations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

1142 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Smith v. State


No. 1210, September Term, 1986


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


71 Md. App. 165; 524 A.2d 117; 1987 Md. App. LEXIS 306



April 20, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Prince George's County, Audrey E. Melbourne, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS REVERSED; CASE REMANDED FOR A NEW TRIAL; COSTS TO BE PAID BY PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for rape, kidnapping, attempted murder, and deadly weapons charges entered by the Circuit Court for Prince George's County (Maryland). Defendant argued that he was denied his right of self-representation and that the trial court misapplied the Maryland Rape Shield Law, specifically Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 461A (1982).


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied defendant's motion to represent himself and granted, pursuant to § 461A, the state's motion to preclude testimony by the victim concerning her prior sexual conduct. The court reversed the convictions, remanded for a new trial, and held that the trial court erred in not allowing defendant to represent himself. The court found that the trial court improperly couched its reasons for finding that defendant was not capable of making a knowing and intelligent waiver of his right to counsel in terms of his ability to carry out an effective defense. The court held that the only relevant inquiry in finding that a proper waiver was made by defendant was whether defendant had the ability to make the knowing and intelligent waiver. However, the court disagreed with defendant's contention that the trial court's preclusion of the victim's testimony as to her prior sexual conduct was an abuse of discretion. The court upheld the trial court's preclusion order and found that the admissibility of the victim's recent sexual activities was limited to cases in which the relevance of the evidence was shown by laying a proper foundation, which defendant failed to do.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions for rape, kidnapping, attempted murder, and deadly weapons charges and remanded the action for a new trial.

Comptroller of Treasury, Income Tax Div. v. NCR Corp.


No. 1132, September Term, 1986


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


71 Md. App. 116; 524 A.2d 93; 1987 Md. App. LEXIS 305



April 16, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Granted August 3, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: TWO APPEALS FROM THE Circuit Court for Baltimore City, John Carroll Byrnes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY WITH DIRECTIONS TO REMAND TO THE MARYLAND TAX COURT FOR THE ENTRY OF AN ORDER CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION; COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLEE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state comptroller assessed additional corporate income tax against appellee taxpayer. The tax court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland) determined that domestic placement interest income and gross-up income were not taxable and that the taxpayer was entitled to modify its sales, payroll, and property factors to reflect worldwide factors. The comptroller appealed.


OVERVIEW: The comptroller contended that the taxpayer improperly deducted both gross-up and domestic placement from its taxable business income, and that the taxpayer should not be permitted to reflect the property, payroll, and sales of its foreign subsidiaries in its apportionment factor. On appeal, the court held that Maryland's tax laws were inextricably keyed to the Internal Revenue Code and that therefore, because I.R.C. § 78 included gross up as part of federal taxable income, it was included in the taxpayer's taxable income for Maryland, as well. The court held that because the domestic placement income was the result of the taxpayer's unitary business, it was also includible in the taxable business income and subject to apportionment under Md. Ann. Code art. 81, § 3106(c) (1980). The court held that because the taxpayer's taxable income did not reflect the actual income earned by any of its foreign subsidiaries, the worldwide values of sales, property, and payroll were properly excluded from the apportionment factor.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals and remanded with instructions to remand to the tax court for entry of an order consistent with the court's opinion.

Crosby v. State


No. 1005, September Term, 1986


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


71 Md. App. 56; 523 A.2d 1042; 1987 Md. App. LEXIS 300



April 14, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City, John C. Brynes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which found him guilty of robbery. The juvenile court had waived jurisdiction over the juvenile to the circuit court pursuant to Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 3-804 (1984 Repl.Vol.). The circuit court denied defendant's motion to transfer jurisdiction back to the juvenile court under Md. Code Ann. art. 27, § 594A (1982 Repl.Vol.).


OVERVIEW: Defendant, 15 years old, was charged as a juvenile with robbery with a deadly weapon. The juvenile court waived jurisdiction over the juvenile to the circuit court pursuant to Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 3-804 (1984 Repl. Vol.), where he was tried as an adult. He was found guilty of robbery with a deadly weapon and the circuit court struck the original verdict and found defendant guilty of robbery. Prior to trial, defendant sought to transfer jurisdiction back to the juvenile court, but the circuit court held it lacked jurisdiction to transfer the matter back. On appeal, the court affirmed the verdict because the circuit court properly denied the motion to grant a waiver back to juvenile court. Md. Code Ann. art. 27, § 594A (1982 Repl. Vol.) was inapplicable as the circuit court acquires jurisdiction only by waiver and did not have original jurisdiction over a juvenile matter.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the circuit court, which found defendant guilty of robbery.

Comptroller of Treasury, Income Tax Div. v. Armco, Inc.


No. 744, September Term, 1986


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


70 Md. App. 403; 521 A.2d 785; 1987 Md. App. LEXIS 272



March 5, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City, Martin B. Greenfeld, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED IN PART AND VACATED IN PART; CASE REMANDED TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY WITH DIRECTIONS TO REMAND TO THE MARYLAND TAX COURT FOR PROCEEDINGS CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION; COSTS TO BE PAID ONE-FOURTH BY ARMCO, INC., AND THREE-FOURTHS BY THE COMPTROLLER OF THE TREASURY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Comptroller of the Treasury (comptroller) sought review of a decision by the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which reversed a tax court decision and held that Md. Code Ann. art. 81, § 280A(c)(7) (1957) (Supp. 1985) discriminated against appellee corporation and violated the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3. The nondomiciliary corporation appealed the ruling that its earned interest income was taxable.


OVERVIEW: The comptroller assessed the dividends from domestic goods exports and interest income as state taxable income. The corporation appealed and contended that §280A(c) (7) violated the Commerce Clause because it limited the exclusion to companies that earned 50 percent of their income in Maryland. It argued that the interest income was not income from a unitary operation. The tax court affirmed the dividend assessment based on lack of subject matter jurisdiction but reversed the assessment of the interest income. The circuit court reversed, holding that § 280A (c)(7) violated the Commerce Clause and that the interest income was state taxable income. The court affirmed the ruling that § 280A (c)(7) violated the Commerce Clause, but vacated the order that the interest income was state taxable income. The court ordered a remand to the tax court for further fact-finding. The court held that: (1) the nondomiciliary corporation had standing to assert the unconstitutionality of § 280A(c)(7) as the statute denied it the tax benefit extended to more closely state-connected corporations; and that (2) evidence related to unitary business activity was inconclusive.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment granted in favor of the corporation as to its claim that a statute that included domestic international sales dividends as state taxable income violated the Commerce Clause. The court vacated the judgment as to the assessment of the corporation's interest income as unitary business activity. The court remanded the case to the circuit court with directions to remand to the tax court for further proceedings.

Ewachiw v. Director of Finance

OVERVIEW: The cash was seized during a search of defendant's residence in which certain equipment and paraphernalia associated with the use and manufacture of controlled dangerous substances was also seized. The trial court granted plaintiff Director of Finance of Baltimore City's (Director) petition to have the cash forfeited under Md. Code art. 27, § 297(a)(6). Goldberg v. State

Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


69 Md. App. 702; 519 A.2d 779; 1987 Md. App. LEXIS 233



January 13, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Howard County, Martin Wolff, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Circuit Court for Howard County (Maryland) that after a non-jury trial convicted him of several counts of fraudulent misappropriation and one count of statutory theft. Defendant's motion to quash the indictment against him was denied.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that he was entitled to a reversal of his convictions because the trial court erred in denying his motion to quash the indictment. Defendant argued that the indictment was invalidated by the attendance of two unauthorized persons before the grand jury. The two persons were a Deputy State Prosecutor and an Assistant State Prosecutor, who had been appointed by State's Attorney for Howard County as Special Assistant State's Attorneys for Howard County . The court rejected defendant's argument. The court held that the State's Attorney for Howard County had the authority to make the appointments and that the two men were not prohibited by constitution, statute or common law from accepting the appointments. The court concluded, that although incompatibility between the offices of Deputy or Assistant State Prosecutor and that of Special Assistant State's Attorney might possibly have invalidated certain actions taken by them on behalf of the State Prosecutor, it did not invalidate the indictment against defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

1148 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


5297 Pulaski Highway, Inc. v. Perryville


No. 379, September Term, 1986


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


69 Md. App. 590; 519 A.2d 206; 1987 Md. App. LEXIS 224



January 8, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Cecil County, Edward D. E. Rollins, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff town requested an injunction to prohibit defendant retail store from selling anything other than lingerie, and the store sought review of the judgment of the Circuit Court, Cecil County (Maryland), which held that the zoning restrictions for adult bookstores in Perryville, Md., Zoning Ordinance No. 84-1, did not violate the First Amendment.


OVERVIEW: The town filed an action against the retail store in which it sought an injunction against the selling of anything other than woman's lingerie. The store asserted that Perryville, Md., Zoning Ordinance No. 84-1, was unconstitutional because it proscribed protected speech. The trial court held that the ordinance was not violative of free speech, and further held that the books and magazines sold by the retail store were obscene. The store was enjoined from the sale of any items other than women's lingerie, until or unless, a conditional use permit was obtained. The store appealed. On appeal, the court held that the record did not reflect that the town enacted the ordinance as a pretext to suppression of protected expression. Further, the court held that the ordinance did not ban adult bookstores, but confined their operation to certain proscribed zones, which was done in the interest of preserving the general welfare of the community. Accordingly, the court held that where the regulation served a legitimate governmental purpose of preserving the general welfare of the community, the town was justified in identifying the store for special treatment or additional restrictions.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court holding that the zoning ordinance was constitutional was affirmed.

 

1149 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Anne Arundel County v. Chu


No. 511, September Term, 1986


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


69 Md. App. 523; 518 A.2d 733; 1987 Md. App. LEXIS 221



January 6, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County, James C. Cawood, Jr., Judge.
ANN ARUNDEL CTY. v. CHU, 1986 Md. App. LEXIS 437 (Md. Ct. Spec. App., Dec. 9, 1986)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED; COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLEES.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county challenged the judgment of the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County (Maryland), which, in appellees', dentists and dental clinic, civil action under Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 551 to recover records that were seized from the clinic, ordered the records to be returned and imposed sanctions.


OVERVIEW: The county police department, pursuant to a search and seizure warrant, seized records from the dental clinic during their fraud investigation. The police believed that the dental clinic was billing the state health insurer for dental work that was never performed. Before any criminal charges were made or the evidence was presented to a grand jury, the dental clinic and dentists sued the county under § 551 to recover the records. The trial court ordered that the records be returned. On appeal, the court reversed. The trial court erred by failing to find that probable cause for the records' seizure was not established as required under § 551 for recovery of property. There was probable cause to seize the records; the warrant application recited a number of instances of demonstrated false claims for dental work that was never done. The trial court erred by treating the § 551 action as tantamount to an invocation of the Fourth Amendment's Exclusionary Rule, as this was not a criminal action.


OUTCOME: The judgment ordering the return of the dental clinic's records and imposing sanctions against the county was reversed.

ANN ARUNDEL CTY. v. CHU


No. 511


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


1986 Md. App. LEXIS 437



December 9, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Substituted opinion at Anne Arundel County v. Chu, 69 Md. App. 523, 518 A.2d 733, 1987 Md. App. LEXIS 221 (1987)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County, James C. Cawood, Jr., Judge



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED; COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLEES.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of the decision of the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County (Maryland), which imposed sanctions against it based on Md. Ann. Code art. 27, §551 in appellees' action for the return of personal property seized pursuant to a search warrant.


OVERVIEW: The county searched appellees' office pursuant to a warrant for the investigation of appellees for fraudulent billing. The county seized some of their business records and before charges were filed, appellees moved for a return of the seized property under the statute. The lower court imposed sanctions for the violation of the statute and the court reversed. The court found that the lower court and the parties' counsel erroneously confused application of the statute, which provided for the return of personal property, with the Fourth Amendment's exclusionary rule. The court determined that the Fourth Amendment was not applicable in the present circumstances because there were no pending criminal charges, and thus there was no criminal defendant who could invoke the exclusionary rule. The court found that the issue was therefore not ripe for scrutiny under the Fourth Amendment. The court concluded that the statute did not contain, explicitly or implicitly, an exclusionary rule and that the case should have been dismissed. The court held that the records did not have to be returned because the provisions of the statute were not satisfied.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's decision to impose sanctions against the county and held that the statute was misapplied.

United Parcel Service, Inc. v. Comptroller of Treasury


No. 497, September Term, 1986


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


69 Md. App. 458; 518 A.2d 164; 1986 Md. App. LEXIS 439



December 9, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From the Circuit Court for Prince George's County, G.R. Hovey Johnson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED; COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLEE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant freight company sought relief from an order entered by the Circuit Court for Prince George's County (Maryland), which held that freight company did not qualify for an exemption, contending that where their vans crossed state lines that they qualified for the exemption.


OVERVIEW: The freight company filed claims for refunds of Maryland sales and use taxes that it alleged to have paid erroneously. The comptroller of the treasury refused to refund $ 98,363.68 of the freight company's claims and assessed an additional $ 38,181.77 for unpaid sales and use taxes plus penalty and accrued interest. The hearing officer for the comptroller affirmed the tax assessment, which was reversed by the tax court. The comptroller appealed to the trial court, which overturned the tax court's determination and reinstated the comptroller's tax assessment and denial of the freight company's requests for refunds. The court concluded that the issue was not whether the vehicles crossed state borders, but whether there was movement of passengers or property across state lines, and it was readily apparent that the freight company qualified for the exemption because the package vans were an integral part of its nationwide delivery system, and the movement of property across state lines placed the freight company's package vans within the ambit of Fed. Tax R. 64. The judgment of the trial court was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which held that the freight company qualified for the exemption.

Schrader v. State


No. 240, September Term, 1986


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


69 Md. App. 377; 517 A.2d 1139; 1986 Md. App. LEXIS 430



December 4, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From The Circuit Court for Montgomery County, Irma S. Raker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED; COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was found guilty of conspiracy in connection with a pyramid scheme. The Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland) sentenced him after a bench trial, to one year imprisonment, which was suspended, five years of supervised probation, and a fine. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The police department received a complaint concerning a business that was owned and operated by defendant. The police department initiated an investigation that included making a telephone call to a number that was on a business flyer. There was a recorded message that indicated that, with a small investment, the return could be from $ 300 to $ 700 per month in approximately three months and that no selling was involved. The officer then attended a couple of meetings that described the scheme. Defendant was charged with conspiracy with the person that conducted the meetings. The person that conducted the meetings was not charged criminally. On appeal, defendant argued that the statute that prohibited pyramids was vague. The court held that the word "primarily" in the statute possessed a common and generally accepted meaning and that there was nothing ambiguous about the term.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment and ordered defendant to pay costs.

 

1153 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Lee v. State


Nos. 913, 914, September Term, 1985


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


69 Md. App. 302; 517 A.2d 774; 1986 Md. App. LEXIS 422



December 3, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Granted March 24, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Two Appeals from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County, John J. Mitchell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS AFFIRMED; COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed their convictions in the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland) for attempted second-degree murder, robbery with a dangerous and deadly weapon, the use of a handgun in the commission of a crime of violence, and the unlawful carrying of a handgun. Defendants argued that their motions to suppress were improperly denied, that the evidence was insufficient, and that a sentence was excessive.


OVERVIEW: A store was robbed at gunpoint by two armed men. The owner was shot, but he saw the assailants leave with cash. Defendants were implicated because of their proximity to the store at the time of the crime, the fact that they were seen spending money shortly thereafter, and a witness's observation of both handling a gym bag containing a handgun. A ballistics test showed that the bullet in the victim's body was fired by the handgun. Defendants were arrested, and motions to suppress evidence were denied by a divided trial court. Defendants were tried for crimes, and convicted. Their appeals of the denial of the motions to suppress were denied by an equally divided court sitting en banc. Defendants appealed, and the court affirmed, holding that reasons for the denial of the suppression motions were unnecessary because the decision was without force as precedent. The evidence was legally sufficient to permit the cases to go to the jury. It was not necessary to address the issue of whether scienter was a required element of the crime of carrying a handgun. The presumptive validity of the decision below was unrequited, and, although the sentence exceeded the guidelines, it was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

1154 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Bratley v. Suburban Bank


No. 813, September Term, 1986


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


68 Md. App. 625; 515 A.2d 236; 1986 Md. App. LEXIS 395



October 8, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Orphan's Court for Montgomery County, Richard B. Latham, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a personal representative of one estate, appealed a judgment from the Orphan's Court for Montgomery County (Maryland). The trial court denied petitioner's request that the trial court compel respondent, the personal representative of another estate, to distribute a legacy to the estate that petitioner represented.


OVERVIEW: A testatrix provided a bequest to a legatee who had handled her financial affairs. The will provided that the bequest would fail if the legatee were no longer alive. The legatee survived the testatrix by 24 days. When respondent learned that the legatee had not survived the testatrix by 30 days pursuant to Md. Code Ann., Est. & Trusts § 4-401, respondent refused to make further distribution to the legatee's estate and demanded a return of the monies it had already paid. Petitioner filed a complaint to compel distribution of the legacy. The trial court denied the petition. The court affirmed. The court agreed that the testatrix had placed a condition of survivorship on the legacy. Under § 4-401, survivorship meant that the legatee was required to survive the testatrix by 30 full days. The anti-lapse statute did not apply to save the legacy because the testatrix had expressly placed a condition of survivorship on the legacy, and the testatrix was presumed to know the common and statutory law when she executed her will. Section 4-401 satisfied the rational basis test and was not unconstitutional; a legatee who died so soon after a testatrix would be unable to enjoy the bequest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that refused to compel distribution of a legacy because the condition of survivorship had not been satisfied.

 

1155 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Davis v. State


No. 1631, September Term, 1985


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


68 Md. App. 581; 514 A.2d 1229; 1986 Md. App. LEXIS 394



September 9, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore County, Cullen H. Hormes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: CONVICTION AFFIRMED. SENTENCE VACATED. CASE REMANDED FOR RESENTENCING CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS TO BE PAID ONE-HALF BY APPELLANT AND ONE-HALF BY BALTIMORE COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland), which convicted him of daytime housebreaking, a violation of Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 30(b), and sentenced him to life imprisonment without the possibility of parole under the provisions of Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 643B(b) as one who had been convicted of and incarcerated for three previous crimes of violence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of daytime housebreaking and was sentenced under § 643B(b) to life imprisonment without the possibility of parole because of his prior convictions for violent crimes. Among other things, defendant challenged the constitutionality of his sentence on Eighth Amendment grounds. The court observed that a criminal sentence had to be proportionate to the crime for which the accused had been convicted. The inclusion of the offense of daytime housebreaking as a predicate violent crime for the purposes of § 643B(b) was not necessarily disproportionate under Eighth Amendment analysis. However, although the crimes committed by defendant were classified as violent crimes, they involved neither violence nor the threat of violence to any person. Moreover, defendant was convicted of stealing items worth little more than $ 100, and of four other convictions the State relied on for sentence enhancement, the two most recent were for stealing items of even lesser value. On the facts of the case, defendant's life sentence without parole violated the Eighth Amendment because he was treated more harshly than criminals convicted of more serious, life-endangering felonies.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction, vacated defendant's sentence, and remanded the case for resentencing consistent with the court's opinion.

 

1156 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Holiday Universal Club, Inc. v. Montgomery County


No. 1160, September Term, 1985


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


67 Md. App. 568; 508 A.2d 991; 1986 Md. App. LEXIS 335



May 15, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Two Appeals from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County, DeLawrence Beard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED; COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant spa owner filed a complaint seeking a declaratory judgment that Montgomery County, Maryland, Public Accommodation Ordinance of the Montgomery County Code § 27-9 was unconstitutional. The owner also sought an interlocutory injunction staying the pending proceedings of appellee, the Human Relations Commission (HRC). The Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland) denied the injunction, and the owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: Members of the owner's spa filed a complaint with the HRC when they were denied admission to a dance class, alleging discrimination in public accommodations. The HRC issued determination letters finding that reasonable grounds existed to believe that the owner violated § 27-9. The HRC scheduled public hearings on the complaints. The owner alleged that the ordinance was unconstitutional either because appellee county lacked the power to enact it or because it was vague, and that the trial court erred in refusing to issue the interlocutory injunction. The court held that the passage of the ordinance was a valid exercise of the county's police power, which was granted to the county by the state. The court did not agree that the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague because § 27-9 very specifically spelled out what acts were prohibited. The court affirmed the denial of the interlocutory injunction, agreeing with the trial court that the owner failed to meet any one of the four standards necessary to be granted the extraordinary remedy of interlocutory relief.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which denied the interlocutory injunction. The court held that the challenged county ordinance was not unconstitutional.

 

1157 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


L.F.M. v. Department of Social Services


No. 877, September Term, 1985


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


67 Md. App. 379; 507 A.2d 1151; 1986 Md. App. LEXIS 314



May 8, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore County, Leonard S. Jacobson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant grandparents sought review of a decision of the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland) finding for appellee city agency that appellants could not seek visitation of their grandchildren. Appellants asserted that they were entitled to be heard as to visitation, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, Md. Const. art. 24, and that the statute not requiring notice of state action was unconstitutional, Md. Code Ann., Fam. Law, § 5-322 (1984).


OVERVIEW: After the parental rights of certain parents were terminated, the children were placed into the adoptive care of strangers. Appellant grandparents asserted that they had a right to be heard in court in their attempt to secure visitation rights concerning their grandchildren, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, Md. Const. art. 24. The court affirmed the lower court's decision for appellee city agency, holding that nothing in state law compelled courts to hear the merits of a petition for visitation by grandparents or other interested persons in a case such as this. The court found that grandparents (unlike parents and their children) had no constitutionally protected liberty interest involving their grandchildren, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, Md. Const. Art. 24. For this reason, the court found that a statute not requiring state notice of action to appellants prior to the termination of the parents' rights, Md. Code Ann., Fam. Law § 5-322 (1984), was not unconstitutional. The court noted that there is a point where adoptive parents can be presumed to be acting in the best interests of their child without having to demonstrate such in court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, finding that the laws of the State of Maryland did not provide a mechanism through which a court could hear the merits of a petition for visitation by all interested persons, including grandparents, in a case involving the termination of parental rights followed by adoption by strangers. The court found that grandparents had no protected liberty interest involving their grandchildren.

Payne v. Prince George's County Dep't of Social Servs.


No. 1133, September Term, 1985


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


67 Md. App. 327; 507 A.2d 641; 1986 Md. App. LEXIS 312



May 7, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Prince George's County, Audrey E. Melbourne, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED. APPELLANT TO PAY THE COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant putative father sought review of a judgment of the Circuit Court for Prince George's County (Maryland), which adjudged him the father of a minor child in a paternity action brought by appellee, Prince George's County Department of Social Services (department), and ordered payment of back support and medical expenses. The putative father argued laches and that the action was time-barred by Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. §5-101.


OVERVIEW: The department filed a paternity proceeding on behalf of a minor child against the putative father, requesting a finding that he was the minor child's father and for an order requiring him to pay child support and for the mother's medical and hospital expenses in connection with the child's birth. The putative father denied paternity and argued that the action was time-barred. The trial court found the putative father to be the minor child's father and ordered him to pay back support and for medical testing. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that: 1) because the two year statute of limitations was unconstitutional, the three year statute of limitations set forth in § 5-101 applied, subject to the tolling provisions of Md. Proc. §5-201; 2) because the putative father's son was a minor when the action was brought, the statute was tolled as to him; and 3) the putative father had not demonstrated prejudice by any delay, nor was there anything in the record that otherwise supported a claim of laches.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in the department's paternity action against the putative father, which adjudicated him to be the father of the minor child and ordered him to pay back support and medical expenses.

State v. One Motor Vehicle to Wit: 1982 Plymouth


No. 873, September Term, 1985


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


67 Md. App. 310; 507 A.2d 633; 1986 Md. App. LEXIS 313



May 7, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City, Martin B. Greenfeld, Judge.



DISPOSITION: ORDER REVERSED; CASE REMANDED FOR PASSAGE OF AN ORDER CONSISTENT WITH THE VIEWS HEREIN EXPRESSED; COSTS TO BE PAID BY LAURA BLASETTI.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state appealed the judgment by the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which found in favor of intervenor owner in the State's forfeiture action.


OVERVIEW: The owner was arrested and marijuana was found in her car. The State filed an action to forfeit the car. The trial court found in favor of the owner and held that the State had failed to prove that the case fell under one of the guidelines set out in Md. Ann. Code art 27, § 297(f). On the State's appeal, the court reversed and remanded. The requirements for forfeiture were set out in art. 27, § 297(a), and the examples set out in § 297(f) were guidelines only, not requirements. When the requirements of § 297(a) were met, the trial court had no discretion to deny forfeiture. Because the requirements of § 297(a) were met, it was error to deny forfeiture.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment, which had found in favor of the owner in the State's forfeiture action. The case was remanded for entry of a judgment in favor of the State.

Baltimore County v. Batza


No. 1089, September Term, 1985


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


67 Md. App. 282; 507 A.2d 216; 1986 Md. App. LEXIS 309



April 10, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] TWO APPEALS FROM THE Circuit Court for Baltimore County, John F. Fader, II, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED; APPELLEES/CROSS-APPELLANTS TO PAY THE COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners and defendant city cross-appealed a decision of the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland) that declared that the legislative determination that the owners bore 52 percent of the cost of sewage line was invalid and remanded the case to the county council for re-determination.


OVERVIEW: A dispute arose from the county's decision to extend public sewerage that would have required the owners who benefited by the extension to ultimately pay for about 52 percent of the cost. The circuit court declared the "legislative determination" that the owners' bear 52 percent of the cost invalid and remanded the case to the county council for a re-determination of the proper amount of the assessment. The court reversed. The court held that the circuit court had jurisdiction to entertain the action, but it erred in the manner it exercised that jurisdiction. The court found that to the extent there was a general public benefit from the extension of the sewer line, it arose from the fact that sewage escaping from those properties would no longer pollute the public drinking water. The court opined that, although the percentage allocation was derived by application of a formula, it was not something that was accepted blindly and unthinkingly. The count concluded that the evidence showed that the county council was aware of the need for the project, the effect of the improvement on both the properties and the public health, and the amount each property would be assessed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the circuit court's decision that the legislative determination that the owners bore 52 percent of the cost of sewage line was invalid.

Middleton v. State


No. 876, September Term, 1985


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


67 Md. App. 159; 506 A.2d 1191; 1986 Md. App. LEXIS 304



April 7, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore County, James S. Sfekas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction by the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland) for attempted daytime housebreaking and malicious destruction of property.


OVERVIEW: A neighbor observed two men trying to pry open the door of a house and called the police. Defendant was identified by the neighbor. Defendant was convicted of attempted daytime housebreaking and malicious destruction of property, and was sentenced as an habitual offender. Defendant appealed, and argued that the evidence was insufficient, that the habitual offender statute, Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 643B(c), and his sentence were unconstitutional, that art. 27, § 643B(c) was not correctly applied, and that the prosecution was barred by double jeopardy. The conviction was affirmed. Because defendant failed to argue at the trial court that there was no proof of an intent to steal, the argument was waived. The propriety of the decision to prosecute defendant as an habitual offender did not depend on whether others who were similarly situated were also so prosecuted. Defendant failed to show any discriminatory purpose in the decision to prosecute him as an habitual offender. Because defendant failed to challenge the validity of the prior convictions used for enhancement, the State was not required to prove that defendant was represented by counsel or that any plea was voluntary.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for attempted daytime housebreaking and malicious destruction of property.

Boyds Civic Asso. v. Montgomery County Council


No. 909, September Term, 1985


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


67 Md. App. 131; 506 A.2d 675; 1986 Md. App. LEXIS 298



April 4, 1986

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff civic association appealed the judgment by the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which dismissed the association's action for an injunction and a declaratory judgment that the amendment of a master plan by defendants district council and planning commission was void.


OVERVIEW: The association was made up of persons who owned property within a master planned district. Trussell v. State


No. 204, September Term, 1985


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


67 Md. App. 23; 506 A.2d 255; 1986 Md. App. LEXIS 292



April 2, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied June 26, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Harford County Cypert O. Whitfill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS AFFIRMED; COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his jury conviction in the Circuit Court for Hartford County (Maryland) for possession of cocaine with intent to distribute, possession of marijuana, and possession of LSD. He raised issues concerning evidentiary matters, juror misconduct, and an improper comment by the circuit court judge on a question asked by the jury.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of possession of cocaine with intent to distribute and possession of marijuana and LSD. The court affirmed and found that the circuit court judge did not abuse his discretion by failing to recuse himself from ruling on defendant's suppression motion merely because the same judge had issued the search warrant under which the evidence in question was seized. In addition, the court rejected his arguments that the affidavit supporting the search warrant did not demonstrate probable cause and that the evidence was not legally sufficient to sustain his convictions. The court found that the circuit court did not err by denying defendant's motion for a mistrial or, in the alternative, to excuse a juror when that juror asked the circuit court where defendant worked and what his source of income was. It was not error for the circuit court judge to answer a juror's question, concerning defendant's relationship with a drug distributor, without first seeking permission from counsel. Md. R. Crim. Causes 4-326(c) required the circuit court only to notify counsel that there had been a communication, not to seek counsel's pre-approval before answering the juror.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for possession of cocaine with intent to distribute, possession of marijuana, and possession of LSD.

Jacobs v. Adams


Nos. 1001, 1010, 1032, September Term, 1985


Court of Special Appeals of Maryland


66 Md. App. 779; 505 A.2d 930; 1986 Md. App. LEXIS 287



March 13, 1986 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] APPEAL NO. 1001 DISMISSED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT. JUDGMENT IN APPEAL NO. 1010 AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT. JUDGMENT IN APPEAL NO. 1032 AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two cases from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland) and one case from the Circuit Court for Prince George's County were consolidated for appeal. The courts ruled that injured plaintiffs were barred from recovering from defendant drivers based on the District of Columbia no-fault insurance law, D.C. Code Ann. § 35-2105. All three accidents took place in the District of Columbia.


OVERVIEW: Preliminarily, the court dismissed one of the appeals for failure to comply with Md. R. App. P. 1028(b) which required that be provided with his appellate brief. He eventually filed a record extract that was well beyond the allowable time, without leave of court, and the court would not accept the belated record extract. On the merits of the case, the court continued to follow the doctrine of lex loci delicti and ruled that the law of the District of Columbia applied to the case insofar as it governed the substantive rights of the parties. The District of Columbia, through the provisions of § 35-2105, had declared that no action existed under the facts of the cases. It did not restrict, limit, define, qualify, or otherwise simply modify the cause of action but declared it to be wholly unavailable. The court found no public policy strong enough to overrule the doctrine of lex loci delicti. It also found that the equal protection guarantees were not violated, because under the rational relation test, the statute bore a rational relationship to a legitimate state purpose in protecting its citizens.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed one appeal for failure to provide the required record extract. It affirmed the decision in favor of the defendants.
